               \n                    Tom Murphy                \n            \n                                    Erykah Badu caught an early break in 1994, opening for emergent artist D’Angelo and adding her music to the foundation of the neo-soul movement. However, it was her 1997 debut album, Baduizm, that truly launched her career and earned her a worldwide audience. Badu’s sultry yet perfectly accented vocals proved to be the ideal vehicle for conveying her rich, wry personal narratives. Had she stopped there, poignantly capturing the universal elements of her own experiences, she would still be an important artist. But Badu’s vision is expansive, and she uses her art as a vehicle for activism as well, famously exemplified by the video for 2010’s “Window Seat,” in which she appears partially nude in Dallas’s Dealey Plaza to make a statement about the perils of groupthink.
